<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome re-sequencing reveals the history of apple and supports a two-stage model for fruit enlargement"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2017 8:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2017-08-15"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2017 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Human selection has reshaped crop genomes. Here we report an apple genome variation map generated through genome sequencing of 117 diverse accessions. A comprehensive model of apple speciation and domestication along the Silk Road is proposed based on evidence from diverse genomic analyses. Cultivated apples likely originate from Malus sieversii in Kazakhstan, followed by intensive introgressions from M. sylvestris. M. sieversii in Xinjiang of China turns out to be an &#8220;ancient&#8221; isolated ecotype not directly contributing to apple domestication. We have identified selective sweeps underlying quantitative trait loci/genes of important fruit quality traits including fruit texture and flavor, and provide evidences supporting a model of apple fruit size evolution comprising two major events with one occurring prior to domestication and the other during domestication. This study outlines the genetic basis of apple domestication and evolution, and provides valuable information for facilitating marker-assisted breeding and apple improvement. Apple is one of the most important fruit crops. Here, the authors perform deep genome resequencing of 117 diverse accessions and reveal comprehensive models of apple origin, speciation, domestication, and fruit size evolution as well as candidate genes associated with important agronomic traits."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Human selection has reshaped crop genomes. Here we report an apple genome variation map generated through genome sequencing of 117 diverse accessions. A comprehensive model of apple speciation and domestication along the Silk Road is proposed based on evidence from diverse genomic analyses. Cultivated apples likely originate from <i>Malus sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan, followed by intensive introgressions from <i>M. sylvestris</i>. <i>M. sieversii</i> in Xinjiang of China turns out to be an âancientâ isolated ecotype not directly contributing to apple domestication. We have identified selective sweeps underlying quantitative trait loci/genes of important fruit quality traits including fruit texture and flavor, and provide evidences supporting a model of apple fruit size evolution comprising two major events with one occurring prior to domestication and the other during domestication. This study outlines the genetic basis of apple domestication and evolution, and provides valuable information for facilitating marker-assisted breeding and apple improvement.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Crop domestication is an artificial selection process, fostering co-dependence between human and crop plants. This process often includes an initial phase of unconscious selection by farmers for several thousand years, followed by a subsequent breeding phase of intentional selection of favorable traits by breeders<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Gross, B. L. &amp; Olsen, K. M. Genetic perspectives on crop domestication. Trends Plant. Sci. &#xA;                           15, 529â537 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d14168e983">1</a></sup>. As a result, cultivated crops diverge from their wild progenitors morphologically and genetically in response to human selection. Recently, rapid advances in sequencing technologies have allowed researchers to sequence entire crop genomes efficiently and the resultant information can help elucidate how human-involved evolutionary processes have shaped modern crop genomes, as demonstrated in peach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Cao, K. et al. Comparative population genomics reveals the domestication history of the peach, Prunus persica, and human influences on perennial fruit crops. Genome Biol. &#xA;                           15, 415 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d14168e987">2</a></sup>, date palm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Hazzouri, K. M. et al. Whole genome re-sequencing of date palms yields insights into diversification of a fruit tree crop. Nat. Commun. &#xA;                           6, 8824 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d14168e991">3</a></sup>, tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Lin, T. et al. Genomic analyses provide insights into the history of tomato breeding. Nat. Genet. &#xA;                           46, 1220â1226 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d14168e995">4</a></sup>, maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Genome-wide genetic changes during modern breeding of maize. Nat. Genet. &#xA;                           44, 812â815 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d14168e999">5</a></sup>, soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Lam, H. M. et al. Resequencing of 31 wild and cultivated soybean genomes identifies patterns of genetic diversity and selection. Nat. Genet. &#xA;                           42, 1053â1059 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1004">6</a></sup>, and rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Xu, X. et al. Resequencing 50 accessions of cultivated and wild rice yields markers for identifying agronomically important genes. Nat. Biotechnol. &#xA;                           30, 105â111 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1008">7</a></sup>. These studies provide valuable information for facilitating future crop improvement and thus securing global food production.</p><p>Cultivated apple (<i>Malus domestica</i> Borkh.), one of the most widely produced and economically important fruit crops in temperate regions, has been domesticated from <i>M. sieversii</i> in the Tian Shan Mountains for 4000â10,000 years, and dispersed from Central Asia to West Europe along the Silk Road, allowing hybridization and introgression of wild crabapples from Siberia (<i>M. baccata</i> (L.) Borkh.), Caucasus (<i>M. orientalis</i> Uglitz.), and Europe (<i>M. sylvestris</i> Mill.)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Cornille, A., Giraud, T., Smulders, M. J., Roldan-Ruiz, I. &amp; Gladieux, P. The domestication and evolutionary ecology of apples. Trends Genet. &#xA;                           30, 57â65 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1031">8</a></sup>. It has been reported that <i>M. sieversii</i> is the primary progenitor and <i>M. sylvestris</i> is a major secondary contributor of cultivated apples<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cornille, A. et al. New insight into the history of domesticated apple: secondary contribution of the European wild apple to the genome of cultivated varieties. PLoS Genet. &#xA;                           8, e1002703 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1041">9</a></sup>. Centuries of human exploitation and selection have produced thousands of apple cultivars with diverse fruit sizes and flavors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Morgan, J. &amp; Richards, A. the New Book of Apples: The Definitive Guide to Over 2,000 Varieties (Ebury Press, 2002)" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1045">10</a></sup>. In addition, apples are characterized with lengthy juvenile phases, self-incompatibility, and vegetative propagation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Cornille, A., Giraud, T., Smulders, M. J., Roldan-Ruiz, I. &amp; Gladieux, P. The domestication and evolutionary ecology of apples. Trends Genet. &#xA;                           30, 57â65 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1049">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Pickersgill, B. Domestication of plants in the Americas: insights from mendelian and molecular genetics. Ann. Bot. &#xA;                           100, 925â940 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1052">11</a></sup>. These characteristics together with its unique domestication history indicate that the impact of domestication on the genomes of apples would be much different from that on the genomes of seed-propagated annual crops as well as self-compatible tree fruits such as peach. However, very limited information is available on how the apple genome has been reshaped by human selections and how small and sour wild ancestors have evolved into modern large and sweet apples. In this study, we performed deep genome sequencing of 117 diverse <i>Malus</i> accessions. The large amount of genomic variation resources has not only allowed us to gain new insights into apple evolution and domestication, but also provided invaluable information for accelerating the usage of wild species in apple breeding and improvement.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome resequencing and variation calling</h3><p>A total of 117 <i>Malus</i> accessions from 24 species were selected for genome sequencing, including 35 <i>M. domestica</i> (24 scion and 11 rootstock cultivars), 10 <i>M. sylvestris</i>, 29 <i>M. sieversii</i>, 9 <i>M. robusta</i>, 6 <i>M. baccata</i>, 4 <i>M. asiatica</i>, 4 <i>M. hupehensis</i>, and 20 in the remaining 17 wild species with one or two accessions per species (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM2">1</a>). Among the 29 <i>M. sieversii</i> accessions, 15 originated in Kazakhstan, on the west side of the Tian Shan Mountains, and 14 were collected from natural forests in Xinjiang of China, on the east side of the Tian Shan Mountains. Six out of the 24 species are native to China, four native to North America, and two to Europe, while some <i>Malus</i> species are considered as intrageneric hybrids, such as <i>M. robusta</i> and <i>M. asiatica</i> (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM3">2</a>). Chinese soft apples, such as âPinpoâ, âXiangguoâ, <i>M. asiatica</i> and <i>M. prunifolia</i> have been cultivated as dessert apples for more than 2000 years in China (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">1</a>). Analysis of the phenotypical data recorded in the USDA-GRIN database (<a href="https://npgsweb.ars-grin.gov/">https://npgsweb.ars-grin.gov/</a>) indicated that domesticated apples are significantly bigger, firmer, and sweeter than <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan, while less acidulated and much bigger than <i>M. sylvestris</i> (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><p>Resequencing of the 117 apple genomes generated a total of 1060âGb high-quality cleaned sequences, with an average of 9.06âGb per accession that represented ~12.2Ã of the apple genome (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">1</a>). After aligning the reads to the pseudo-haplotype apple genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus x domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. &#xA;                           42, 833â839 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1148">12</a></sup> (v1.0p), we identified a final set of 7,218,060 single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">2</a> and Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">2</a>). Furthermore, we identified 431,597 small insertions and deletions (indels). Polymerase chain reaction (PCR) amplification and Sanger sequencing on genomic regions containing 958 randomly selected SNP loci in six apple accessions indicated a high accuracy rate (98.1%) for our genotype calling.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">An apple evolutionary map</h3><p>We first examined the phylogeny among wild and cultivated apples by constructing a neighbor-joining phylogenetic tree with pear as the outgroup using SNPs at fourfold degenerate sites (4D SNPs). The tree showed that accessions of <i>M. domestica</i> and its introgression contributor <i>M. sylvestris</i> formed a subclade within a large mixed clade comprising <i>M. sieversii</i> accessions that are considered as progenitors of cultivated apples<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Cornille, A., Giraud, T., Smulders, M. J., Roldan-Ruiz, I. &amp; Gladieux, P. The domestication and evolutionary ecology of apples. Trends Genet. &#xA;                           30, 57â65 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1178">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cornille, A. et al. New insight into the history of domesticated apple: secondary contribution of the European wild apple to the genome of cultivated varieties. PLoS Genet. &#xA;                           8, e1002703 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1181">9</a></sup>, while accessions of other wild species position outside this domestication-related clade (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig1">1a</a>). Wild species native to North America (<i>M. ioensis</i>, <i>M. angustifolia</i>, <i>M. fusca</i>, and <i>M. coronaria</i>) are the closest to pear, followed by Asian wild species <i>M. baccata</i> and <i>M. hupehensis</i>. Notably, the recent introgression from <i>M. sylvestris</i> into <i>M. domestica</i> has been so intensive that the cultivated apples now appear to be closer to European crabapple <i>M. sylvestris</i> than to their progenitor <i>M. sieversii</i>, which is consistent with a previous report<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cornille, A. et al. New insight into the history of domesticated apple: secondary contribution of the European wild apple to the genome of cultivated varieties. PLoS Genet. &#xA;                           8, e1002703 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1220">9</a></sup>. A principal component analysis (PCA) illustrated a similar pattern to the phylogenetic tree in that <i>M. domestica</i>, <i>M. sieversii</i>, and <i>M. sylvestris</i> accessions formed closely related clusters that were clearly separated from the dispersed accessions of other wild species (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig1">1b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-00336-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_336_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-00336-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_336_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Population structure of 117 domesticated and wild apples. <b>a</b> Neighbor-joining phylogenetic tree constructed using SNPs at fourfold degenerate sites. Each species group is color coded, with <i>red squares</i> representing rootstocks and <i>red dots</i> scions. <b>b</b> Principal component analysis (<i>PCA</i>) of the 117 apple accessions. <b>c</b> Bayesian model-based clustering of the 117 apple accessions with the number of ancestry kinship (<i>K</i>) from 3 to 5. Each <i>vertical bar</i> represents one apple accession and the <i>x</i> axis shows different apple accessions. Each <i>color</i> represents one putative ancestral background and the <i>y</i> axis quantifies ancestry membership. Asi <i>M. asiatica</i>, Bac <i>M. baccata</i>, Dom <i>M. domestica</i>, Hup <i>M. hupehensis</i>, <i>Other</i> other wild species, Rob <i>M. robusta</i>, Sie_K <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan, Sie_X <i>M. sieversii</i> in Xinjiang, Syl <i>M. sylvestris</i>
                                    </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>To further understand the evolutionary history of apple, we used a Bayesian clustering algorithm with admixed models<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Hubisz, M. J., Falush, D., Stephens, M. &amp; Pritchard, J. K. Inferring weak population structure with the assistance of sample group information. Mol. Ecol. Resour &#xA;                           9, 1322â1332 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1322">13</a></sup> to estimate ancestry proportions for each accession (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig1">1c</a>). Our Î<i>K</i> analysis revealed that five populations (<i>K</i>â=â5) represent the best model for these 117 accessions (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">2</a>
                           <b>)</b>. For <i>K</i>â=â3, <i>M. domestica</i> and its wild relatives, <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i>, were clearly separated from other wild species, supporting the evolutionary history of domesticated apple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Cornille, A., Giraud, T., Smulders, M. J., Roldan-Ruiz, I. &amp; Gladieux, P. The domestication and evolutionary ecology of apples. Trends Genet. &#xA;                           30, 57â65 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1354">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cornille, A. et al. New insight into the history of domesticated apple: secondary contribution of the European wild apple to the genome of cultivated varieties. PLoS Genet. &#xA;                           8, e1002703 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1357">9</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Harris, S. A., Robinson, J. P. &amp; Juniper, B. E. Genetic clues to the origin of the apple. Trends Genet. &#xA;                           18, 426â430 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1360">14</a></sup>. With <i>K</i> from 4 to 5, two new subpopulations arose from the wild species other than <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i>, indicating their high diversity and further distance from domesticated apples. <i>M. sieversii</i> accessions from the two sides of the Tian Shan Mountains segregated into two different subpopulations reflecting their geographical distributions. <i>M. sieversii</i> accessions in Kazakhstan showed admixed ancestry possibly from hybridizations with wild apples such as <i>M. orientalis</i> along the Silk Road, and/or domesticated apples cultivated nearby, while Xinjiang accessions kept their homogeneous genetic background probably due to their geographical isolation that blocks interspecific hybridization. In addition to <i>M. sieversii</i> in Xinjiang, six other species in distinct habitats, such as <i>M. ioensis</i> and <i>M. angustifolia</i> in North America and <i>M. baccata</i> in East Asia, were also identified with homogeneous genetic background, which gave rise to other hybrid species and possess tremendous value in apple breeding practices (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">3</a>). The structure of several hybrid species was consistent with their known pedigrees recorded in the USDA-GRIN database, including <i>M. asiatica</i>, <i>M. prunifolia</i>, <i>M. robusta</i>, and several rootstocks, while that of <i>M. platycarpa</i> did not agree with its pedigree in the database (Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">5</a> and Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">3</a>). Together, these findings prompt us to propose a comprehensive apple evolutionary map across Eurasia continent, illustrating the initial domestication from <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan, the hybridization between <i>M. sylvestris</i> and the ancient domesticated apples spread from Central Asia to Europe via the Silk Road westward, and the rise of orient hybrid species from crosses between <i>M. baccata</i> and <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan distributed and cultivated along the Silk Road eastward (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig2">2a</a> and Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">4</a>). During the domestication process, cultivated apples retained the large fruit size from <i>M. sieversii</i>, gained the firm texture and appetizing flavor from the hybridization with <i>M. sylvestris</i> and continued to be bred into larger and firmer fruit with better flavor and aroma.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-00336-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_336_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-00336-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_336_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Apple evolutionary map. <b>a</b> Apple evolutionary map along the west and east bounds of the Silk Route with center of origin at Kazakhstan in central Asia. <b>b</b> Decay of linkage disequilibrium (<i>LD</i>) measured as the squared correlation coefficient (<i>r</i>
                                       <sup>2</sup>) by pairwise physical distance in <i>M. domestica</i>, <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan, <i>M. sieversii</i> in Xinjiang, <i>M. sylvestris</i>, and other wild species. <b>c</b> Multidimensional scaling (<i>MDS</i>) plot for the pairwise <i>F</i>
                                       <sub>ST</sub> matrix. The Euclidean distances between each pair of groups significantly represent the corresponding <i>F</i>
                                       <sub>ST</sub> values (Spearman rank-sum correlation <i>Ï</i>â=â0.95; <i>p</i>â&lt;â10<sup>â14</sup>). <b>d</b> Major alleles of scion and rootstock cultivars derived from <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan and <i>M. sylvestris</i>. Asi <i>M. asiatica</i>, Bac <i>M. baccata</i>, Dom <i>M. domestica</i>, Hup <i>M. hupehensis</i>, Rob <i>M. robusta</i>, Sie_K <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan, Sie_X <i>M. sieversii</i> in Xinjiang, Syl <i>M. sylvestris</i>
                                    </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>We then evaluated the genetic diversity of different apple subpopulations. The genome-wide nucleotide diversity (<i>Ï</i>) of <i>M. domestica</i> (2.20âÃâ10<sup>â3</sup>) was lower than that of <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan (2.35âÃâ10<sup>â3</sup>), <i>M. sylvestris</i> (2.55âÃâ10<sup>â3</sup>), and other wild species (4.26âÃâ10<sup>â3</sup>), while <i>M. sieversii</i> in Xinjiang exhibited the lowest diversity level (1.30âÃâ10<sup>â3</sup>). Compared to other domesticated perennial crops, the nucleotide diversity in cultivated apple is higher than that of peach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Cao, K. et al. Comparative population genomics reveals the domestication history of the peach, Prunus persica, and human influences on perennial fruit crops. Genome Biol. &#xA;                           15, 415 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1582">2</a></sup> (1.5âÃâ10<sup>â3</sup>), but lower than that of cassava<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Kawuki, R. S., Ferguson, M., Labuschagne, M., Herselman, L. &amp; Kim, D.-J. Identification, characterisation and application of single nucleotide polymorphisms for diversity assessment in cassava (Manihot esculenta Crantz). Mol. Breeding &#xA;                           23, 669â684 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1589">15</a></sup> (2.6âÃâ10<sup>â3</sup>) and date palm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Hazzouri, K. M. et al. Whole genome re-sequencing of date palms yields insights into diversification of a fruit tree crop. Nat. Commun. &#xA;                           6, 8824 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1595">3</a></sup> (9.2âÃâ10<sup>â3</sup>). The similar level of nucleotide diversity between <i>M. domestica</i> and its progenitor <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan indicated a very weak bottleneck, if any, during apple domestication, consistent with findings in previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Cornille, A., Giraud, T., Smulders, M. J., Roldan-Ruiz, I. &amp; Gladieux, P. The domestication and evolutionary ecology of apples. Trends Genet. &#xA;                           30, 57â65 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1608">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cornille, A. et al. New insight into the history of domesticated apple: secondary contribution of the European wild apple to the genome of cultivated varieties. PLoS Genet. &#xA;                           8, e1002703 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1611">9</a></sup>. Linkage disequilibrium (LD) analyses for each group further supported a very weak and nearly undetectable domestication bottleneck (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig2">2b</a> and Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">5</a>). The rapid LD decay of domesticated apple suggested that a large set of markers densely covering the genome is preferred for a high-resolution population genetic analysis, as indicated in a recent study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Migicovsky, Z. et al. Genome to phenome mapping in apple using historical data. Plant Genome &#xA;                           9, doi:&#xA;                    10.3835/plantgenome2015.11.0113&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1621">16</a></sup>. Here we also demonstrated the power of our high-density SNPs in enhancing the resolution of genome-wide association studies (GWAS) (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">6</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">6</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">3</a>, and Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM4">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM5">4</a>).</p><p>To further investigate population divergence among different species groups, we computed pairwise <i>F</i>
                           <sub>ST</sub> values, demonstrating consistent relationships among the subpopulations with our proposed evolutionary scenario (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig2">2c</a> and Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">7</a>). Genome-wide inference of major allele origins in <i>M. domestica</i> from <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan or <i>M. sylvestris</i> revealed that 46% of the <i>M. domestica</i> genome was probably derived from its progenitor <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan and 21% from its secondary contributor <i>M. sylvestris</i>, while the origin of the remaining 33% was uncertain. The genomic introgression from <i>M. sylvestris</i> to scion cultivars is about 10% higher than that to rootstock cultivars, raising the possibility that <i>M. sylvestris</i> may have contributed important alleles for fruit quality and production traits to dessert apple cultivars (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig2">2d</a> and Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Differential selection of domesticated apples</h3><p>Considering the remarkable role of <i>M. sylvestris</i> in shaping modern domesticated apples, genomic regions dramatically affected by selection during apple domestication were identified in two contrasts: <i>M. domestica</i> vs. <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan for initial domestication (<i>Dom_SieK</i>) and <i>M. domestica</i> vs. <i>M. sylvestris</i> for secondary introgression (<i>Dom_Syl</i>) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig3">3</a> and Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">10</a>). The selected regions of <i>Dom_SieK</i> and <i>Dom_Syl</i> had a mean size of 33.1 and 42.8âkb, covered a total length of 13.9âMb (3.7 % of genome) and 17.2âMb (4.6%), and harbored 840 and 1089 genes, respectively, among which 246 (29.3%) and 336 (30.9%) showed differential expression during apple fruit development (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM6">5</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM9">8</a>). The identified selective sweeps are enriched with genes associated with fruit sugar content, firmness, color, hormone, and secondary metabolism in both <i>Dom_SieK</i> and <i>Dom_Syl</i> contrasts, while genes related to fruit acidity were only enriched in <i>Dom_Syl</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig3">3</a> and Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM10">9</a>). Notably, the enriched genes included those encoding six sugar transporters, several key enzymes in the glycolysis/gluconeogenesis pathway, two sucrose synthases and three cellulose synthases in <i>Dom_SieK</i>, two aluminum-activated malate transporters, a malate dehydrogenase, and a citrate synthase in <i>Dom_Syl</i>, and two sucrose synthases, one pyruvate decarboxylase, and one cellulose synthase in both <i>Dom_SieK</i> and <i>Dom_Syl</i>, highlighting the constant selection of sweet and firm fruits in the history of apple domestication (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig3">3</a>). Together, these candidate domestication-related genes are indicative of different selective forces for improving different agronomic traits from the two wild contributors during domestication.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-00336-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_336_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-00336-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_336_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Genome-wide distribution of selective sweeps in <i>M. domestica</i>. <b>a</b> Selective sweeps in <i>M. domestica</i> compared with <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan. <b>b</b> Selective sweeps in <i>M. domestica</i> compared with <i>M. sylvestris</i>. XP-CLR scores are plotted across the 17 chromosomes in the apple genome with key functional enzyme genes labeled above the dot peaks. <i>Red vertical boxes</i> illustrate selective sweeps, and <i>blue boxes</i> represent local GO enriched regions with associated traits labeled below. Selective regions shared by both comparisons are shaded with <i>light blue bars</i>, while interesting regions only identified in one of the two comparisons are shaded with <i>light yellow bars</i>. Traits include fruit acidity (A), color (C), firmness (F), hormone (H), soluble sugar (S), and secondary metabolites (M). Gene abbreviations: <i>ALMT</i> aluminum-activated malate transporter, <i>ACO</i> 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylate oxidase, <i>ACS</i> 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylate synthase, <i>AE</i> aldose 1-epimerase, <i>AR</i> aldose reductase, <i>BG</i> beta-galactosidase, <i>CAS</i> cycloartenol synthase, <i>CTS</i> citrate synthase, <i>CLS</i> cellulose synthase, <i>FH</i> flavanone 3-hydroxylase, <i>GA3OX</i> gibberellin 3-beta-dioxygenase, <i>GG</i> glucan endo-1,3-b-glucosidase, <i>GMD</i> GDP-mannose 4,6-dehydratase, <i>IFR</i> isoflavone reductase, <i>IMS</i>, 2-isopropylmalate synthase, <i>MD</i> malate dehydrogenase, <i>PDC</i> pyruvate decarboxylase, <i>PDK</i> pyruvate dehydrogenase kinase, <i>PE</i> pectin esterase, <i>PFK</i> 6-phospho-fructokinase, <i>PG</i> polygalacturonase, <i>PL</i> pectate lyase, <i>SPD</i> sorbitol 6-phosphate dehydrogenase, <i>SPS</i> sucrose phosphate synthase, <i>SS</i> sucrose synthase, <i>ST</i> sugar transporter</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>We further scanned SNPs that were highly divergent between <i>M. domestica</i> and different wild species groups using the top 1% <i>F</i>
                           <sub>ST</sub> values (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM11">10</a>). A large number of disease resistance (R) genes and genes involved in various different abiotic stresses were found to contain non-synonymous SNPs highly divergent between <i>M. domestica</i> and other wild species groups, suggesting their adaptations to different growth environments. Within <i>M. domestica</i> accessions, genes underlying dwarf quantitative trait loci (QTLs) (<i>Dw1</i> and <i>Dw2</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Foster, T. M., Celton, J.-M., ChagnÃ©, D., Tustin, D. S. &amp; Gardiner, S. E. Two quantitative trait loci, Dw1 and Dw2, are primarily responsible for rootstock-induced dwarfing in apple. Hortic. Res. &#xA;                           2, 15001 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1934">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Fazio, G. et al. Dw2, a new dwarfing locus in apple rootstocks and its relationship to induction of early bearing in apple scions. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. &#xA;                           139, 87â98 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1937">18</a></sup>, R genes and receptor kinase genes were identified to have non-synonymous SNPs highly divergent between rootstock and scion cultivars, which could facilitate the study of important apple rootstock traits, such as dwarfing, precocity, and disease resistance (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM12">11</a>). The highly divergent SNPs and the associated genes discovered in this study provided ample information for broadening our understanding of apple speciation, differentiation, and evolution.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Increase of fruit size prior to and during apple domestication</h3><p>One essential aspect of domestication process for most crop species is to increase fruit and/or seed size, which is also referred to as âdomestication syndromeâ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Doebley, J. F., Gaut, B. S. &amp; Smith, B. D. The molecular genetics of crop domestication. Cell &#xA;                           127, 1309â1321 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1952">19</a></sup>. Fruit sizes of <i>M. sylvestris</i> are significantly smaller than those of both <i>M. domestica</i> and <i>M. sieversii</i>, while domesticated apples have larger fruits than <i>M. sieversii</i> (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">1</a>d, e). Two previously reported fruit weight QTLs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Devoghalaere, F. et al. A genomics approach to understanding the role of auxin in apple (Malus x domestica) fruit size control. BMC Plant Biol. &#xA;                           12, 1â15 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d14168e1972">20</a></sup> (designated as <i>fw1</i> on chromosome 15 and <i>fw2</i> on chromosome 8) were found to be co-located with selective sweeps (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig4">4a</a>). QTLs <i>fw1</i> and <i>fw2</i> harbor 11 and 7 genes, respectively, in selective regions of <i>M. domestica</i> from <i>M. sieversii</i>, and 8 and 21 genes, respectively, in sweeps from <i>M. sylvestris</i> (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM13">12</a>). Genes encoding regulators of cell division, such as <i>fw2.2</i> in tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Frary, A. et al. fw2.2: a quantitative trait locus key to the evolution of tomato fruit size. Science &#xA;                           289, 85â88 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d14168e2008">21</a></sup> and <i>CNR1</i> in maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Guo, M. et al. Cell Number Regulator1 affects plant and organ size in maize: implications for crop yield enhancement and heterosis. Plant Cell &#xA;                           22, 1057â1073 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d14168e2015">22</a></sup>, have been reported to control organ size, and recently a Î²-galactosidase gene was found to be involved in the regulation of fruit weight and size in strawberry, besides fruit softening<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Paniagua, C. et al. Antisense down-regulation of the strawberry Î²-galactosidase gene FaÎ²Gal4 increases cell wall galactose levels and reduces fruit softening. J. Exp. Bot. &#xA;                           67, 619â631 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d14168e2019">23</a></sup>. We found that one cell division regulatory gene (MDP0000223854) and two Î²-galactosidase genes (<i>MDP0000921848</i> and <i>MDP0000179821</i>) in <i>fw1</i> were under human selection. Interestingly, the Î²-galactosidase gene, <i>MDP0000179821</i>, was in selected regions of both <i>Dom_SieK</i> and <i>Dom_Syl</i> and showed differential expression during apple fruit development with highest expression at the active cell division stage (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM13">12</a>). Furthermore, several cell division regulatory genes (e.g., <i>MDP0000555176</i>, <i>MDP0000802780</i>, <i>MDP0000846861</i>, and <i>MDP0000681201</i>) and a gene (<i>MDP0000241347</i>) homologous to rice <i>GS3</i> that controls grain size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Takano-Kai, N. et al. Evolutionary history of GS3, a gene conferring grain length in rice. Genetics &#xA;                           182, 1323â1334 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d14168e2064">24</a></sup> were found in several other selected regions, and they all showed highest expression at the active cell division stage of fruit development (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM7">6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM9">8</a>), suggesting their potential contribution to the increase of fruit size during apple domestication.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-00336-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_336_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-00336-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_336_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Evolution of fruit size during speciation and domestication in apple. <b>a</b> Domestication sweeps underlying apple fruit size QTLs. Within the physical intervals (<i>orange boxes</i>) of the two fruit weight QTLs <i>fw1</i> and <i>fw2</i>, distributions of XP-CLR scores are shown. Selective sweeps are marked with <i>red bars</i> and interesting genes are labeled above peaks. <b>b</b> 
                                       <i>MiRNA172g/miRNA172h</i> and the two target genes that contain highly divergent SNPs (pointed by <i>arrows</i>) between <i>M. domestica</i> with large fruits and other wild species bearing very small fruits. <b>c</b> Schematic diagram of the two-step evolution of apple fruit size. <i>BG</i> beta-galactosidase, <i>FBP</i> fructose-1,6-bisphosphatase, <i>FD</i> ferredoxin, <i>PPD</i> pyruvate phosphate dikinase, <i>P4</i> patellin-4, <i>UGE</i> UDP-glucose 4-epimerase</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>
                           <i>MiRNA172p</i> was recently reported to regulate apple fruit size by targeting AP2 transcription factors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Yao, J.-L. et al. A microRNA allele that emerged prior to apple domestication may underlie fruit size evolution. Plant J. &#xA;                           84, 417â427 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d14168e2150">25</a></sup>. Using their precursor sequences, the 16 miRNA172 genes identified in apple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Xia, R., Zhu, H., An, Y. Q., Beers, E. P. &amp; Liu, Z. Apple miRNAs and tasiRNAs with novel regulatory networks. Genome Biol. &#xA;                           13, R47 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d14168e2154">26</a></sup> were clustered into four clades (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">11</a>
                           <b>)</b>. We detected a highly differentiated SNP only between domesticated and other wild apples in the precursor sequences of <i>miRNA172g</i> and <i>miRNA172h</i>, two miRNAs sharing the same precursor sequences (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig4">4b</a>). No differentiated SNPs were detected in the 16 miRNA172 genes between <i>M. domestica</i> and <i>M. sieversii</i>. Furthermore, we found that five out of the eight target AP2 genes of <i>miRNA172g</i> and <i>miRNA172h</i> carried highly differentiated SNPs in the comparison between <i>M. domestica</i> and other wild species, two of which comprised non-synonymous SNPs (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig4">4b</a> and Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">11</a>
                           <b>)</b>. Therefore, besides the previously discovered <i>miRNA172p</i>
                           <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Yao, J.-L. et al. A microRNA allele that emerged prior to apple domestication may underlie fruit size evolution. Plant J. &#xA;                           84, 417â427 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d14168e2203">25</a></sup>, we identified two additional miRNA172 genes (<i>miRNA172g</i> and <i>miRNA172h</i>) that might have contributed to the increase of fruit size during <i>Malus</i> speciation prior to domestication.</p><p>Taken together, we propose a two-step evolution model for fruit size enlargement in apple to characterize its unique evolution process (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig4">4c</a>). Unlike modern maize and tomato whose domestication process started with ancestral species bearing very small seeds or fruits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Lin, T. et al. Genomic analyses provide insights into the history of tomato breeding. Nat. Genet. &#xA;                           46, 1220â1226 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d14168e2222">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Genome-wide genetic changes during modern breeding of maize. Nat. Genet. &#xA;                           44, 812â815 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d14168e2225">5</a></sup>, apple domestication was initiated from <i>M. sieversii</i> whose fruits are larger than all other wild apples<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Cornille, A., Giraud, T., Smulders, M. J., Roldan-Ruiz, I. &amp; Gladieux, P. The domestication and evolutionary ecology of apples. Trends Genet. &#xA;                           30, 57â65 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14168e2232">8</a></sup>. As fruit size is one of the most desirable traits for crop domestication and improvement, apple domestication started with a great advantage and much lower evolutionary pressure than other crops. The finding of fruit weight QTLs, and <i>miRNA172s</i>, and their target genes from comparisons between small-fruited wild apples and large-fruited cultivars helped explain why a weak selection in a highly heterozygous perennial crop can still yield favorable large fruits. The mild increase in fruit size during several thousand years of domestication after speciation, partially contributed by QTLs <i>fw1</i> and <i>fw2</i>, suggests that apple fruit size has great potential to be increased in future breeding practices, considering that the modern tomato fruit is approximately 100 times larger than its direct wild progenitor.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Enhancement of fruit firmness during apple domestication</h3><p>Besides large fruits, humans also have been selecting firm flesh texture, not only for crispy taste, but also for a longer shelf life, better post-harvest disease resistance, and reduced bruising during harvest and transportation. Fruit firmness is directly linked to enzyme-mediated cell wall modification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Brummell, D. A. Cell wall disassembly in ripening fruit. Funct. Plant Biol. &#xA;                           33, 103â119 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d14168e2255">27</a></sup>. To decipher the genetic mechanism underlying the selection for firm apples, we mined selective sweeps for genes that were potentially involved in regulating fruit firmness. We found a region on chromosome 16 was under intensive human selection in the <i>Dom_SieK</i> contrast, harboring genes encoding key cell wall modifying enzymes including three polygalacturonases (<i>MDP0000512850</i>, <i>MDP0000939625</i>, and <i>MDP0000873268</i>), and one glucan endo-1,3-beta-glucosidase (<i>MDP0000295938</i>) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig5">5a</a>). Furthermore, several other selective regions were also found to contain genes related to cell wall modifications. For example, one selective sweep on chromosome 17 from the <i>Dom_SieK</i> comparison comprised three cellulose synthase genes (<i>MDP0000190520</i>, <i>MDP0000289339</i>, and <i>MDP0000184309</i>), and another sweep from the same chromosome harbored one pectate lyase (<i>MDP0000301545</i>), one glucan endo-1,3-beta-glucosidase (<i>MDP0000206670</i>), and one aldose 1-epimerase (<i>MDP0000737131</i>) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig5">5b</a>). Similarly, another sweep on chromosome 12 from the <i>Dom_Syl</i> contrast harbored one endo-beta-1,4-mannase (<i>MDP0000832632</i>) and two pectinesterase genes (<i>MDP0000278119</i> and <i>MDP0000278118</i>) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig5">5c</a>). A number of these cell wall-related genes were differentially regulated during fruit development (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM7">6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM9">8</a>). Therefore, the evolution of these genes might have contributed to the firm fruit texture of domesticated apples.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-00336-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_336_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-00336-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_336_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Domestication sweeps underlying apple fruit firmness. Distributions of XP-CLR scores and nucleotide diversity (Ï) in selective regions on chromosomes 6 (<b>a</b>) and 17 (<b>b</b>) in <i>M. domestica</i> from <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan (<i>Dom_SieK</i>) and chromosome 12 (<b>c</b>) from <i>M. sylvestris</i> (<i>Dom_syl</i>), which harbor genes known to be associated with fruit firmness. Distributions of XP-CLR scores are shown in <i>top panels</i> with selective sweeps marked with <i>red bars</i> and interesting genes labeled above peaks. Distributions of Ï are shown in <i>bottom panels</i> with <i>M. domestica</i> in <i>orange lines</i>, and <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i> in <i>green lines</i>. <i>AE</i> aldose 1-epimerase, <i>CLS</i> cellulose synthase, <i>GG</i> glucan endo-1,3-b-glucosidase, <i>MM</i> endo-beta-1,4-mannase, <i>PE</i> pectinesterase, <i>PG</i> polygalacturonase, <i>PL</i> pectate lyase</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Enrichment of fruit flavor during apple domestication</h3><p>Fruit flavor, mainly a balance between sugars and acids, is another important trait under human selection. Although it has been reported that fruit acidity rather than sweetness is likely to undergo artificial selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Ma, B. et al. Comparative assessment of sugar and malic acid composition in cultivated and wild apples. Food Chem. &#xA;                           172, 86â91 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d14168e2424">28</a></sup>, we found that both fruit sugar content and acidity have been altered during apple domestication using historical phenotype data in the USDA-GRIN database (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">1</a>). During the domestication from <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan, a region on chromosome 12 has undergone intensive selection and co-localizes with a sorbitol QTL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Kunihisa, M. et al. Identification of QTLs for fruit quality traits in Japanese apples: QTLs for early ripening are tightly related to preharvest fruit drop. Breed Sci. &#xA;                           64, 240â251 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d14168e2434">29</a></sup>. Interestingly, genes encoding four sorbitol transporters and two sugar transporters cluster within this sorbitol QTL under selection, and all four sorbitol transporter genes were differentially expressed during apple fruit development (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig6">6a</a> and Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM14">13</a>). Similarly, another genomic region on chromosome 13 enriched with genes encoding key enzymes of sugar metabolism is under intensive selection from <i>M. sylvestris</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig6">6b</a>). Notably, the two sucrose synthase genes in this region are also in selective sweeps from <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Fig. 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-00336-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_336_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-00336-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_336_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Domestication of fruit sweetness and acidity in apples. <b>a</b> Selective sweeps from <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan that co-localize with a sorbitol QTL. <b>b</b> Domestication sweeps from <i>M. sylvestris</i> that contain key genes for sugar metabolism. Distributions of XP-CLR scores are shown. Selective sweeps are marked with <i>red bars</i> and interesting genes are labeled above peaks. <b>c</b>â<b>f</b> Domestication of the <i>Ma1</i> gene that regulates apple fruit acidity. <b>c</b> Distributions of nucleotide diversity (<i>Ï</i>) of <i>M. domestica</i> (<i>red</i>), <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan (<i>blue</i>), and <i>M. sylvestris</i> (<i>green</i>) in the <i>Ma1</i> genome region. <b>d</b> 
                                       <i>Ma1</i> selective sweeps during domestication from <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan (<i>blue</i>) and <i>M. sylvestris</i> (<i>green</i>). Sweep regions are marked with <i>filled boxes</i>. <b>e</b> Distribution of <i>F</i>
                                       <sub>ST</sub> between <i>M. domestica</i> and <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan in the <i>Ma1</i> genome region. <b>f</b> Distribution of <i>F</i>
                                       <sub>ST</sub> between <i>M. domestica</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i> in the <i>Ma1</i> genome region. <i>bHLH</i> transcription factor, <i>CTS</i> citrate synthase, <i>HK</i> hexokinase, <i>MYB</i> transcription factor, <i>PDK</i> pyruvate dehydrogenase kinase, <i>PE</i> pectin esterase, <i>PG</i> polygalacturonase, <i>PK</i> pyruvate kinase, <i>SBT</i> sorbitol transporter, <i>SS</i> sucrose synthase, <i>ST</i> sugar transporter</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>To study fruit acidity during apple domestication, we investigated the nucleotide diversity of gene <i>Ma1</i>, which underlies the major fruit acidity QTL <i>Ma</i> and encodes an aluminum-activated malate transporter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Bai, Y. et al. A natural mutation-led truncation in one of the two aluminum-activated malate transporter-like genes at the Ma locus is associated with low fruit acidity in apple. Mol. Genet. Genomics &#xA;                           287, 663â678 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d14168e2628">30</a></sup>. We identified a region around 1.5âkb upstream of <i>Ma1</i> coding sequence (CDS) with substantially reduced nucleotide diversity in <i>M. domestica</i> compared to <i>M. sylvestris</i>, and another region about 750âbp downstream of <i>Ma1</i> CDS when compared to <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig6">6</a>câf), indicating that the <i>Ma1</i> might be under strong selection during apple domestication. The distributions of <i>F</i>
                           <sub>ST</sub> of SNPs surrounding the <i>Ma1</i> gene region further underpinned the selective sweep regions. In addition to <i>Ma1</i>, two other aluminum-activated malate transporter genes and a gene encoding malate dehydrogenase were identified in selection sweeps from <i>M. sylvestris</i>, assisting in explaining the significantly reduced acidity level in domesticated apples when compared to <i>M. sylvestris</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#Fig3">3</a> and Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#MOESM1">1</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>We report here a comprehensive genome variation map of apple through deep resequencing the genomes of 117 wild and cultivated apples. The population analyses reveal that modern cultivated apples originate from <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan with intensive recent introgressions from <i>M. sylvestris</i>, while <i>M. sieversii</i> in Xinjiang is an isolated ecotype with a homogeneous genetic background that holds great potential for future apple improvement. Chinese native species, such as <i>M. asiatica</i> and <i>M. prunifolia</i>, are very likely to be hybrids between <i>M. baccata</i> and <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan. The similar level of genetic diversity between <i>M. domestica</i> and its direct wild progenitor <i>M. sieversii</i> in Kazakhstan is consistent with the comparably fast LD decays in these two species and an undetectable bottleneck in apple domestication. The weak selection during apple domestication might be explained by its unique physiological characteristics, such as self-incompatibility, lengthy juvenile phases, and limited sexual reproduction. However, several thousand years of selection still has accumulated a considerable amount of desirable fruit traits that make apple one of the most important fruit crops worldwide. We identified selective sweeps underlying fruit size, flesh firmness, sweetness, and acidity, as well as highly divergent SNPs between rootstock and scion cultivars that are associated with dwarfing and disease resistance. The identified large set of SNPs, highly confident candidate genes associated with important apple traits, and the insight gained regarding apple evolution and domestication provide valuable information for facilitating future apple marker assisted breeding, QTL mapping, and population-structured association studies.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Sample preparation and sequencing</h3><p>A total of 117 apple accessions were included in this study, of which 70 were provided by USDA-ARS Plant Genetics Resources Unit (Geneva, NY, USA), 33 by Institute of Pomology of Chinese Academy of Agricultural Sciences (Xingcheng, Liaoning, China), and 14 were collected from the Tian Shan Mountains (Xinjiang, China). Genomic DNA was extracted from young leaves using the DNeasy Plant Mini Kit (Qiagen, Venlo, the Netherlands). Paired-end Illumina genomic libraries were prepared and sequenced on an Illumina Hiseq 2500 platform following the manufacturerâs instructions (Illumina, San Diego, CA).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Mapping and variant calling</h3><p>The Illumina paired-end reads from each apple accession were first processed to collapse duplicated read pairs into unique read pairs. Duplicated read pairs were defined as those having identical bases at positions of 15-90 in both left and right reads. The resulting unique reads were processed to remove adaptor and low-quality sequences using Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           30, 2114â2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d14168e2736">31</a></sup>. The cleaned unique reads were aligned to the apple reference genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus x domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. &#xA;                           42, 833â839 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d14168e2740">12</a></sup> (v1.0p, <a href="https://www.rosaceae.org/species/malus/malus_x_domestica/genome_v1.0p">https://www.rosaceae.org/species/malus/malus_x_domestica/genome_v1.0p</a>) using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with BurrowsâWheeler transform. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d14168e2751">32</a></sup>, and only uniquely mapped reads were retained. Following mapping, genotypes were assigned to each genomic position for each accession based on the alignment mpileup files generated by SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-017-00336-7#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d14168e2755">33</a></sup>. SNPs and small indels in the population of 117 apple accessions were then identified if they were supported by at least two mapped reads, and had an allele frequency â¥0.3. Furthermore, SNP sites with the nearest SNPs less than 5âbp away, containing only heterozygous SNPs, or with more than 50% missing data were excluded.</p><p>To validate the SNP calling, we randomly selected 958 homozygous SNP loci across the genome and PCR-amplified and Sanger-sequenced the corresponding genomic regions in six apple accessions (<i>M. domestica</i>, Dom_01; <i>M. sieversii</i>, Sie_K_04, Sie_X_03 and Sie_X_09; <i>M. sylvestris</i>, Syl_01; and <i>M. hupehensis</i>, Hup_01).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Phylogenetic and population structure analyses</h3><p>We selected a total of 24,326 SNPs at fourfold degenerate sites (4D SNPs) with minor allele frequency (MAF) â¥5% and missing rate per site â¤10% for phylogenetic and population structure analyses.
